<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Xavier Argout and colleagues report the draft genome of Theobroma cacao, the tropical crop that is the source of chocolate. The sequence assembly covers approximately 80% of the genome. We sequenced and assembled the draft genome of Theobroma cacao, an economically important tropical-fruit tree crop that is the source of chocolate. This assembly corresponds to 76% of the estimated genome size and contains almost all previously described genes, with 82% of these genes anchored on the 10 T. cacao chromosomes. Analysis of this sequence information highlighted specific expansion of some gene families during evolution, for example, flavonoid-related genes. It also provides a major source of candidate genes for T. cacao improvement. Based on the inferred paleohistory of the T. cacao genome, we propose an evolutionary scenario whereby the ten T. cacao chromosomes were shaped from an ancestor through eleven chromosome fusions."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>We sequenced and assembled the draft genome of <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, an economically important tropical-fruit tree crop that is the source of chocolate. This assembly corresponds to 76% of the estimated genome size and contains almost all previously described genes, with 82% of these genes anchored on the 10 <i>T. cacao</i> chromosomes. Analysis of this sequence information highlighted specific expansion of some gene families during evolution, for example, flavonoid-related genes. It also provides a major source of candidate genes for <i>T. cacao</i> improvement. Based on the inferred paleohistory of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome, we propose an evolutionary scenario whereby the ten <i>T. cacao</i> chromosomes were shaped from an ancestor through eleven chromosome fusions.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Theobroma cacao</i> L. is a diploid tree fruit species (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 20 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Davie, J.H. Chromosome studies in the Malvaceae and certain related families. II. Genetica 17, 487–498 (1935)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d52119e1365">1</a>)) endemic to the South American rainforests. Cocoa was domesticated approximately 3,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Henderson, J.S., Joyce, R.A., Hall, G.R., Hurst, W.J. &amp; McGovern, P.E. Chemical and archaeological evidence for the earliest cacao beverages. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 104, 18937–18940 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d52119e1369">2</a></sup> in Central America<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Coe, S.D. &amp; Coe, M.D. The True History of Chocolate. (Thames and Hudson Ltd., London, England, 1996)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d52119e1373">3</a></sup>. The Criollo cocoa variety, having a nearly unique and homozygous genotype, was among the first to be cultivated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Motamayor, J.C. et al. Cacao domestication I: the origin of the cacao cultivated by the Mayas. Heredity 89, 380–386 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d52119e1378">4</a></sup>. Criollo is now one of the two cocoa varieties providing fine flavor chocolate.</p><p>However, due to its poor agronomic performance and disease susceptibility, more vigorous hybrids created with foreign (Forastero) genotypes have been introduced. These hybrids, named Trinitario, are now widely cultivated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Motamayor, J.C., Risterucci, A.M., Heath, M. &amp; Lanaud, C. Cacao domestication II: progenitor germplasm of the Trinitario cacao cultivar. Heredity 91, 322–330 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d52119e1385">5</a></sup>. Here we report the sequence of a Belizean Criollo plant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Mooleedhar, V., Maharaj, W. &amp; O'Brien, H. The collection of Criollo cocoa germplasm in Belize. Cocoa Grower's Bull. 49, 26–40 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d52119e1389">6</a></sup>.</p><p>Consumers have shown an increased interest for high-quality chocolate, and for dark chocolate, containing a higher percentage of cocoa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Cocoa Resources in consuming Countries–ICCO Market Committee, 10th meeting. EBRD Offices London, MC 10, 16 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d52119e1396">7</a></sup>. Fine-cocoa production is nevertheless estimated to be less than 5% of the world cocoa production due to the low productivity and disease susceptibility of the traditional fine-flavor cocoa varieties. Therefore, breeding of improved Criollo varieties is important for sustainable production of fine-flavor cocoa.</p><p>3.7 million tons of cocoa are produced annually (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Sec10">URLs</a>). However, fungal, oomycete and viral diseases, as well as insect pests, are responsible for an estimated 30% of harvest losses (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Sec10">URLs</a>). Like many other tropical crops, knowledge of <i>T. cacao</i> genetics and genomics is limited. To accelerate progress in cocoa breeding and the understanding of its biochemistry, we sequenced and analyzed the genome of a Belizean Criollo genotype (B97-61/B2). This genotype is suitable for a high-quality genome sequence assembly because it is highly homozygous as a result of the many generations of self-fertilization that occurred during the domestication process.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We used a genome-wide shotgun strategy incorporating Roche/454, Illumina and Sanger sequencing technologies. The International Cocoa Genome Sequencing consortium (ICGS) produced a total of 17.6 million 454 single reads, 8.8 million 454 paired end reads, 398.0 million Illumina paired end reads and about 88,000 Sanger BAC end reads, corresponding to 26 Gb of raw data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Tables 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Figs. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">2</a>). We used the Roche/454 and Sanger raw data to produce the assembly. This represented ×16.7 coverage of the 430-Mb genome of B97-61/B2, whose size was estimated by flow cytometry (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 3</a>).</p><p>This assembly, performed with Newbler software (Roche, Inc.), consists of 25,912 contigs and 4,792 scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Tab1">Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Eighty percent of the assembly is in 542 scaffolds, and the largest scaffold measures 3.4 Mb. We determined the N50 (the scaffold size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence assembly can be found) to be 473.8 kb. The total length of the assembly was 326.9 Mb, which represents 76% of the estimated genome size of the <i>T. cacao</i> genotype B97-61/B2 (430 Mb). In addition, we used a high coverage of Illumina data (×44 coverage of the genome), which has a different error profile than 454 data, to improve accuracy of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome sequence (Online Methods).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Global statistics of the genome assembly and annotation of <i>Theobroma cacao</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.736/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The resulting assembly appears to cover a very large proportion of the euchromatin of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome. We confirmed the high genome coverage of this assembly by comparing it to the unigene resource (38,737 unigenes assembled from 715,457 expressed sequence tag (EST) sequences from the B97-61/B2 genotype). We recovered 97.8% of the unigene resource in the <i>T. cacao</i> genome assembly.</p><p>Using a set of 1,259 molecular markers from a consensus genetic linkage map established from two mapping populations, 94% of the markers (1,192) were unambiguously located on the assembly, allowing us to anchor 67% of the assembled 326 Mb along the ten <i>T. cacao</i> linkage groups. The remaining 33% of the genome assembly was in 2,207 scaffolds. Fifty percent of the assembly could be anchored and oriented (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). The average ratio of genetic-to-physical distance was 1 cM per 444 kb in centromeric regions and 1 cM per 146 kb in distal chromosomal regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Gene content and repeated sequences annotation</h3><p>We performed the identification and annotation of transposable elements using a two step approach: the first approach was based on the <i>de novo</i> identification of transposable elements from the assembled scaffolds and the second one was based on the search for transposable elements from the unassembled reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>This <i>de novo</i> search lead to the identification of a total of 67,575 transposable element–related sequences in the assembled cocoa sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The second step led to the identification of three highly repeated transposable element families from the unassembled reads dataset. The most common transposable element was a long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposon that we named Gaucho. It is a Copia-like element 11,297 bp in length that is repeated approximately 1,100 times, based upon its occurrences in the 454 unassembled sequences. Fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization (FISH) analysis revealed that Gaucho is distributed on all chromosome arms but is found mainly in their median region (as opposed to the centromeric and telomeric regions), a classical feature shared by many LTR retrotransposons in plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). The other two highly repeated families were another Copia-like LTR retrotransposon and a Mu-type transposon.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="FISH analysis of T. cacao chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: FISH analysis of <i>T. cacao</i> chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.736/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.736/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng736_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.736/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng736_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="283"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) <i>In situ</i> hybridization of <i>T. cacao</i> chromosomes stained with DAPI (blue) using a ThCen repeat probe (red). (<b>b</b>) <i>In situ</i> hybridization using Gaucho LTR retrotransposon (green) and ThCen repeat (red) probes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.736/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Class I elements were the most abundant, representing 69% of the total transposable elements in the cocoa genome, with a total of 290 Gypsy-like and 159 Copia-like families. In addition, we identified 36 transposons and 1,353 miniature inverted-repeat transposon element (MITE) families (class II) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 6</a>). The most highly repeated transposon families were Mutator and Vandal (Mu type), with copy numbers of 994 and 1,978, respectively. Transposable elements were particularly abundant in centromeric regions, as illustrated in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig2">Figure 2</a>; this feature was already observed in other sequenced genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2037">8</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="T. cacao genome heat map."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: <i>T. cacao</i> genome heat map.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.736/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.736/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng736_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.736/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng736_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="400"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The ten <i>T. cacao</i> chromosomes harboring 11 chromosome fusions (in black dotted boxes) identified in these genomes are illustrated according to their ancestral chromosomal origin (see paleo-chromosome color code in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). Centromeres are marked 'Cent'. For the ten chromosomes, heat maps are provided for the CDS (blue &lt;60%, yellow 60%–90% and red &gt;90%), class I and II transposable elements (blue &lt;80%, yellow &gt;80% and red <span class="stix">∼</span>100%), ThCen and Gaucho elements (blue &lt;50% of maximum, yellow ≥50% of maximum and red = maximum) and telomeric repeats (blue = 0, yellow &lt;40% and red &gt;40%). Only the elements present in the assembled part of the genome are represented. Therefore, the genome distribution of the repeated sequences represented in this figure could be biased due to the major limitations of <i>de novo</i> sequencing of complex genomes using next-generation sequencing (NGS), which is limited in its ability to assemble highly repeated sequences.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.736/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Altogether, the transposable elements identified in both assembled (84 Mb) and un-assembled (20.3 Mb) reads represent about 24% of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome. This value is substantially lower than that for other sequenced genomes of similar size, for example, rice (35%, for 380 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2079">9</a></sup> and grape (41.4%, for 475 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2083">10</a></sup>. However, sequencing and assembling of highly repeated sequences can be expected to be the major limitation of <i>de novo</i> sequencing of a complex genome using next-generation sequencing. This is particularly true for transposon element families that have undergone very recent amplification, like in the case of Gaucho. Therefore, we conclude that the total contribution of repetitive elements to the whole cocoa genome may be underestimated.</p><p>In addition, we identified a tandem repeat sequence (that we named ThCen) from the 454 repeat reads. This tandem repeat is 212 bp long and, when used as probe in a FISH experiment, was found to be located in the centromeres of all cocoa chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Tandem repeats and retrotransposons are the major components of plant centromeres<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wolfgruber, T.K. et al. Maize centromere structure and evolution: sequence analysis of centromeres 2 and 5 reveals dynamic loci shaped primarily by retrotransposons. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000743 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2097">11</a></sup>. The copy number of Gaucho and ThCen repeat sequences varied up to 2.5-fold among <i>T. cacao</i> genotypes from various genetic origins. We observed a positive Pearson correlation (<i>r</i> = 0.56) between genome size and ThCen repeat copy number, suggesting a possible contribution of ThCen repeats in genome size variation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 7</a>).</p><p>We annotated the genome sequence using the integrative gene prediction package EUGene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Foissac, S. et al. Genome annotation in plants and fungi: EuGène as a model platform. Curr. Bioinform. 3, 87–97 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2120">12</a></sup> following specific training for <i>T. cacao</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>). Homology searching and functional annotation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>) led to the identification of 28,798 <i>T. cacao</i> protein-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Tab1">Table 1</a>), with an average gene size of 3,346 bp and a mean of 5.03 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 8</a>). Compared to the smaller <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> genome, the <i>T. cacao</i> genome has a higher gene number, a similar exon number per gene and a lower mean gene density per 100 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 8</a>). The genes in <i>T. cacao</i> were more abundant in subtelomeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>), as previously observed in other sequenced plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2159">13</a></sup>.</p><p>The comparison of cocoa, <i>A. thaliana</i>, grape, soybean and poplar proteomes revealed 6,362 clusters of genes (totaling 52,176 genes) distributed among all five eudicot genomes and 682 gene families (totaling 2,053 genes) specific to the cocoa genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Most of these 682 gene families encode hypothetical proteins, as supported at the transcript level. The functional analysis of these five proteomes using gene ontology terms revealed a similar pattern among them (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). A specific feature of the <i>T. cacao</i> clusters common to the other species was the relatively high level of metabolic and cellular processes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Figs. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">7</a>). On the other hand, grape- and cocoa-specific clusters showed the highest unknown-function percentages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Venn diagram showing the distribution of shared gene families among Theobroma cacao, Arabidopsis thaliana, Populus trichocarpa, Glycine max and Vitis vinifera."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Venn diagram showing the distribution of shared gene families among <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Populus trichocarpa, Glycine max</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.736/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.736/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng736_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.736/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng736_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="696"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Numbers in parentheses indicate the number of genes in each cluster. The Venn diagram was created with web tools provided by the Bioinformatics and Systems Biology of Gent (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Sec10">URLs</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.736/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>MicroRNAs (miRNAs) are short noncoding RNAs that regulate target genes transcriptionally or post-transcriptionally. Many of them play important roles in development and stress responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Voinnet, O. Origin, biogenesis, and activity of plant microRNAs. Cell 136, 669–687 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2240">14</a></sup>. A total of 83 <i>T. cacao</i> miRNAs from 25 families were computationally predicted based on sequence similarity to known plant miRNAs in miRBase 14 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Griffiths-Jones, S., Saini, H.K., van Dongen, S. &amp; Enright, A.J. miRBase: tools for microRNA genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D154–D158 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2246">15</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Figs. 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">9</a>). Ninety-one <i>T. cacao</i> miRNA targets were predicted. Most predicted targets were homologous to known miRNA targets in other plant species, but there was a profound bias toward putative transcription factors compared to the other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 11</a>), suggesting that miRNAs are major regulators of gene expression in <i>T. cacao</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Disease resistance–related genes</h3><p>Fungal and oomycete diseases are a major constraint to world cocoa production, and the search for natural disease resistance is one of the main objectives of all <i>T. cacao</i> breeding programs. Resistance genes (<i>R</i> genes) are divided into 2 classes: <i>NBS-LRR</i>, the nucleotide-binding site leucine-rich repeat class of genes, and <i>RPK</i>, the receptor protein kinase class of genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Afzal, A.J., Wood, A.J. &amp; Lightfoot, D.A. Plant receptor-like serine threonine kinases: roles in signaling and plant defense. Mol. Plant Microbe Interact. 21, 507–517 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2292">16</a></sup>.</p><p>Within the <i>RPK</i> class, one family of plant-specific transmembrane receptors was shown to also possess <i>LRR</i>-class genes in its extracellular domain and have important roles in defense responses or in plant development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Diévart, A. &amp; Clark, S.E. LRR-containing receptors regulating plant development and defense. Development 131, 251–261 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2305">17</a></sup>. The <i>LRR-RLK</i> family consists of more than 200 members in <i>A. thaliana</i> and more than 400 members in poplar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lehti-Shiu, M.D., Zou, C., Hanada, K. &amp; Shiu, S.H. Evolutionary history and stress regulation of plant receptor-like kinase/pelle genes. Plant Physiol. 150, 12–26 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2316">18</a></sup>. Here we show that the <i>T. cacao</i> genome contains at least 253 <i>LRR-RLK</i> genes orthologous to <i>Arabidopsis LRR-RLK</i> genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>). Reports indicate that the <i>LRR-RLK</i> family is divided into 19 subfamilies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lehti-Shiu, M.D., Zou, C., Hanada, K. &amp; Shiu, S.H. Evolutionary history and stress regulation of plant receptor-like kinase/pelle genes. Plant Physiol. 150, 12–26 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2336">18</a></sup>. In Viridiplantae, some of these subfamilies have expanded dramatically. As in <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, the <i>LRR-XII</i> subfamily has greatly expanded in the <i>T. cacao</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 13</a>), with approximately 36% of the <i>LRR</i> genes belonging to this subfamily.</p><p>The <i>NBS</i> genes, encoding nucleotide-binding site proteins, also play an important role in resistance to pathogens and in the cell cycle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="DeYoung, B.J. &amp; Innes, R.W. Plant NBS-LRR proteins in pathogen sensing and host defense. Nat. Immunol. 7, 1243–1249 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2368">19</a></sup>. The <i>NBS</i> gene family is rather abundant in plant genomes, ranging from 0.6% to approximately 2% of the total gene number (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>We identified a total of 297 non-redundant <i>NBS</i>-encodingorthologous genes in the <i>T. cacao</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Figs. 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">11</a>). Among these genes, one class, characterized by the <i>TIR</i> (encoding the toll interleukin receptor) motif, is markedly underrepresented in the <i>T. cacao</i> genome compared to other eudicot plants, with only 4% of <i>NBS</i> orthologous genes containing <i>TIR</i> motifs, in contrast to grape, poplar, Medicago (20%) or <i>Arabidopsis</i> (65%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 14</a>). The <i>TIR</i> motifs have been shown to be present in basal angiosperms and eudicots, but are nearly absent in monocots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tarr, D.E.K. &amp; Alexander, H.M. TIR-NBS-LRR genes are rare in monocots: evidence from diverse monocot orders. BMC Res. Notes 2, 197 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2422">20</a></sup>. It has been suggested that the <i>TIR-NBS-LRR</i> resistance genes are more ancient than the divergence of angiosperm and gymnosperms and that they have been lost in the cereal genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Pan, Q., Wendel, J. &amp; Fluhr, R. Divergent evolution of plant NBS-LRR resistance gene homologues in dicot and cereal genomes. J. Mol. Evol. 50, 203–213 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2429">21</a></sup>. Their lower level in the cocoa genome compared to other eudicots, and the close relatedness of cocoa to a common eudicot ancestor as shown by paleohistory studies (see below), suggests a divergent evolution of <i>NBS-TIR</i> orthologous cocoa genes from an ancestral locus, leading to a lower expansion of this gene family in <i>T. cacao</i>.</p><p>Another gene family that plays a major role in plant defense is the <i>NPR</i> gene family. <i>NPR1</i> is an <i>Arabidopsis</i> BTB/POZ domain protein that acts as a central mediator of the plant defense signal transduction pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Mukhtar, M.S., Nishimura, M.T. &amp; Dangl, J. NPR1 in plant pefense: it's not over 'til it's turned over. Cell 137, 804–806 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2453">22</a></sup>. We surveyed the <i>T. cacao</i> genome sequence and found four related <i>T. cacao</i> orthologous genes corresponding to each of the <i>NPR1</i> subfamilies found in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). Recently, we showed that one of these genes (located on chromosome 9) is a functional ortholog of <i>Arabidopsis NPR1</i> by transgenic complementation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Shi, Z., Maximova, S., Lui, Y., Verica, J. &amp; Guiltinan, M.J. Functional analysis of the Theobroma cacao NPR1 Gene in Arabidopsis. BMC Plant Biol. 10, 248 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2480">23</a></sup>.</p><p>We mapped the <i>NBS</i>, <i>LRR-LRK</i> and <i>NPR1</i> orthologous genes along the <i>T. cacao</i> pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). They were distributed across the ten chromosomes, with a large number being organized in clusters, as is classically observed for these classes of genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Lehmann, P. Structure and evolution of plant disease resistance genes. J. Appl. Genet. 43, 403–414 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2506">24</a></sup>.</p><p>A meta-analysis of quantitative trait loci (QTL) related to disease resistance previously identified in <i>T. cacao</i> was recently done<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Lanaud, C. et al. A meta–QTL analysis of disease resistance traits of Theobroma cacao L. Mol. Breed. 24, 361–374 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2516">25</a></sup>. We compared the QTL genetic localizations found in this previous study with the distribution of <i>NBS-LRR</i>, <i>LRR-LRK</i> and <i>NPR</i> orthologous cocoa genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Considering an average confidence interval of about 20 cM for the 76 QTLs identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Lanaud, C. et al. A meta–QTL analysis of disease resistance traits of Theobroma cacao L. Mol. Breed. 24, 361–374 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2533">25</a></sup>, most of the QTLs are located in genome regions containing candidate resistance genes. However, due to the fact that a large number of QTLs and candidate resistance genes are widespread across the genome, many colocalizations may have occurred at random. Therefore, the candidate genes potentially underlying QTLs need to be further studied by functional genomics approaches to confirm their potential roles in disease resistance in cocoa.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genes potentially involved in cocoa qualities</h3><p><i>T. cacao</i> seeds are fermented, dried and then processed into cocoa mass (ground, dehusked seeds), cocoa butter (triacylglycerol storage lipids from cotyledonary endosperm cells) and cocoa powder (defatted mass consisting primarily of cell walls, endosperm storage proteins, starch and proanthocyanins, as well as other flavonoids, aromatic terpenes, theobromine and many other metabolites). In order to characterize the gene families involved in cocoa quality traits, we used a translational approach to survey the <i>T. cacao</i> genome using molecular and biochemical knowledge from <i>Arabidopsis</i>, poplar, grape and other model plant species.</p><p>Oils, proteins, starch and various secondary metabolites such as flavonoids, alkaloids and terpenoids comprise the principal molecular components of cocoa affecting flavor and quality. Storage lipids (triacylglycerols) provide carbon and energy reserves for germinating cocoa embryos (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). <i>T. cacao</i> seed storage lipids (cocoa butter), representing 50% of the dry seed weight, are exceptional in their very high level of stearate (30–37%), which gives cocoa butter its relatively high melting point (34–38 °C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Griffiths, G. &amp; Harwood, J.L. The regulation of triacylglycerol biosynthesis in cocoa (Theobroma cacao) L. Planta 184, 279–284 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2562">26</a></sup>. The unique fatty acid profile of cocoa butter enhances the olfactory qualities of chocolate and confectionaries and makes it valuable for cosmetic and pharmaceutical products. We discovered a total of 84 orthologous <i>T. cacao</i> genes potentially involved in lipid biosynthesis, which is 13 more than those discovered in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Beisson, F. et al. Arabidopsis genes involved in acyl lipid metabolism. A 2003 census of the candidates, a study of the distribution of expressed sequence tags in organs, and a web-based database. Plant Physiol. 132, 681 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2571">27</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 15</a>). Consistent with the large amount of storage lipids produced in <i>T. cacao</i> seeds, the genome contained five additional genes encoding acyl-ACP thioesterase fat B (<i>FATB</i>) and three additional genes encoding ketoacyl-ACP synthase, the two key workhorse enzymes leading to the synthesis of triacylglycerols.</p><p>Flavonoids are a diverse group of plant secondary metabolites that play many important roles during plant development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Pourcel, L., Routaboul, J., Cheynier, V., Lepiniec, L. &amp; Debeaujon, I. Flavonoid oxidation in plants: from biochemical properties to physiological functions. Trends Plant Sci. 12, 29–36 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2588">28</a></sup>. They are involved in plant defense against insects, pathogens and microbes, in absorption of free radicals and ultraviolet light, and in attraction of beneficial symbionts and pollinators. Proanthocyanidins are flavonoid polymers that are present in large amounts in <i>T. cacao</i> seeds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). Recent evidence suggests that proanthocyanidins may be beneficial to human health by improving cardiovascular health, providing cancer chemopreventative effects and also through neuroprotective activities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Spencer, J.P. Flavonoids and brain health: multiple effects underpinned by common mechanisms. Genes Nutr. 4, 243–250 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2598">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Rimbach, G., Melchin, M., Moehring, J. &amp; Wagner, A.E. Polyphenols from cocoa and vascular health-a critical review. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 10, 4290–4309 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2601">30</a></sup>. We identified 96 <i>T. cacao</i> genes orthologous to <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes that are involved in the flavonoid biosynthetic pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 16</a>), which is 60 more than are present in <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Of these genes, we evaluated the function of <i>TcANS</i>, <i>TcANR</i> and <i>TcLAR</i> in transgenic <i>Arabidopsis</i> and Tobacco, demonstrating that they are functional orthologs of <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Liu, Y. Molecular analysis of genes involved in the synthesis of proanthocyanidins in theobroma cacao. Thesis 1–146 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2634">31</a></sup>. Notably, although <i>Arabidopsis</i> has only one gene encoding dihydroflavonol-4-reductase (<i>DFR</i>), the <i>T. cacao</i> genome contains 18 orthologous <i>DFR</i> genes. DFR catalyzes the reaction that produces the flavan-3,4-diols, the immediate precursors of the flavonoids catechin and epicatachin. These compounds can accumulate to as much as 8% of the dry <i>T. cacao</i> seed, making <i>T. cacao</i> one of the richest known sources of this phytonutrient<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Tomas-Barberan, F.A. et al. A new process to develop a cocoa powder with higher flavonoid monomer content and enhanced bioavailability in healthy humans. J. Agric. Food Chem. 55, 3926–3935 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2657">32</a></sup>.</p><p>Terpenoids constitute a large family of natural compounds and play diverse roles in plants as hormones, pigments and in plant-environment interactions and defense. They are major components of resins, essential oils and aromas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Liu, Y., Wang, H., Ye, H. &amp; Li, G. Advances in the plant isoprenoid biosynthesis pathway and its metabolic engineering. J. Integr. Plant Biol. 47, 769–782 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2664">33</a></sup>. Among them, two subclasses of terpenoids are particularly involved in aromas: monoterpenes (C10), which represent aromatic compounds that are the basis of floral essences and essential oils, and sesquiterpenes (C15), which may also constitute a defense response of plants toward microorganisms or insect aggression. Compared to bulk cocoa, a higher level of monoterpenes (such as linalool, an acyclic monoterpene alcohol found in the floral scent of <i>Clarkia breweri</i> and of many other plants species) has been observed in fine-flavored cocoa varieties like Criollo and Nacional, which are characterized by fruity and floral notes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Ziegleder, G. Linalol contents as characteristics of some flavour grade cocoas. Z. Lebensm. Unters. Forsch. 191, 306–309 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2671">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Chanliau, S. &amp; Cros, E. Influence du traitement post-récolte et de la torréfaction sur le développement de l'arôme cacao. 12th Int. Cocoa Res. Conf., Salvador de Bahia (Brazil) 959–964 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2674">35</a></sup>.</p><p>We identified 57 <i>T. cacao</i> genes that are orthologs of <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes that encode terpene synthase (<i>TPS</i>), which catalyze terpenoid synthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Liu, Y., Wang, H., Ye, H. &amp; Li, G. Advances in the plant isoprenoid biosynthesis pathway and its metabolic engineering. J. Integr. Plant Biol. 47, 769–782 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2691">33</a></sup>, and nine pseudogenes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 17</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Figs. 16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">17</a>). This number is higher than in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and poplar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2708">36</a></sup>, which have 30 and 40 genes, respectively, and lower than in grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2712">10</a></sup>, which has 89 functional genes. The classification of <i>TPS</i>s in the different subclasses revealed that 34% of them correspond to monoterpenes and 31% correspond to sesquiterpenes. Two gene families are particularly expanded in <i>T. cacao</i>: the linalool synthase family (monoterpenes), which is represented by 7 genes clustered in a region of chromosome 6, and the cadinene synthase family (sesquiterpenes), comprising 10 members, among which 7 are localized in a same region of chromosome 7. Cadinene synthase is one of the key enzymes involved in the synthesis of gossypol, a toxic terpenoid produced in the seeds of cotton, a species belonging to Malvaceae, the same family as <i>T. cacao</i>. In cocoa, cadinene synthase has been found to be expressed in pod tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Argout, X. et al. Towards the understanding of the cocoa transcriptome: production and analysis of an exhaustive dataset of ESTs of Theobroma cacao generated from various tissues and under various conditions. BMC Genomics 9, 512 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2726">37</a></sup> in response to attacks by mirids (<i>Sahlbergella singularis)</i>, a major insect pest of cocoa trees in Africa. Therefore, the cadinene synthase orthologous genes are candidates for elements of the cocoa insect resistance response.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Colocalization of quality related genes and QTL</h3><p>Previous studies have reported QTLs associated with quality traits such as lipid and flavonoid content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Lanaud, C. et al. Identification of QTLs related to fat content, seed size and sensorial traits in Theobroma cacao L. Proc. 14th Int. Cocoa Res. Conf. 13–18 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2742">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Araújo, I.S. et al. Mapping of quantitative trait loci for butter content and hardness in cocoa beans (Theobroma cacao L.). Plant Mol. Bio. Rep. 27, 177–183 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2745">39</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>). For most of these QTLs, genes encoding key enzymes of these biosynthetic pathways were found to be colocalized with most of these QTLs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>). For example, a major QTL for fat content is associated on chromosome 9 with a gene orthologous to <i>KCS</i>, encoding beta ketoacyl-CoA synthase, and is located very close to an ortholog of one member of the <i>FATB</i> gene group, which is specifically expanded in <i>T. cacao.</i> A strong QTL for cocoa butter hardness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Araújo, I.S. et al. Mapping of quantitative trait loci for butter content and hardness in cocoa beans (Theobroma cacao L.). Plant Mol. Bio. Rep. 27, 177–183 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2765">39</a></sup> was found localized in linkage group 7 near a gene orthologous to <i>FAD4</i>, which is involved in creating a bond between C2 and C3 of the lipid chain, resulting in lipids with a higher melting point, which, in terms of cocoa butter, represents greater hardness.</p><p>Similarly, we found each of the QTLs for astringent taste to be closely associated with genes potentially involved in the proanthocyanidins biosynthetic pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). For example, two genes orthologous to that encoding flavonoid 3-hydroxylase (<i>F3H</i>) and one orthologous to that encoding dihydro-flavonol-4-reductase (<i>DFR</i>), which are specifically expanded in <i>T. cacao</i>, co-localize on linkage group 1 with a major astringency QTL.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8"><i>T. cacao</i> genome paleo-history</h3><p>Angiosperms have been shown to evolve through rounds of paleo-polyploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2798">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2801">40</a></sup>. Two types of events have been reported in the literature for eudicots: an ancestral event (referenced as γ) and lineage-specific events (referenced as α and β). In order to investigate the paleo-history of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome, we characterized shared paleo-polyploidies based on the integration of orthologous relationships identified between <i>T. cacao</i> and five eudicot sequenced genomes (<i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2813">41</a></sup>, grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2817">10</a></sup>, poplar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2822">36</a></sup>, soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2826">42</a></sup> and papaya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991–996 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2830">43</a></sup>), as well as paralogous relationships identified between the ten <i>T. cacao</i> chromosomes.</p><p>Recently, we published a method for the identification of orthologous regions between plant genomes as well as for the detection of duplicated blocks within genomes based on integrative sequence alignment criteria combined with statistical validations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Salse, J., Abrouk, M., Murat, F., Quraishi, U.M. &amp; Feuillet, C. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Briefings Bioinf. 10, 619–630 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2840">44</a></sup>. This approach has been recently applied to available monocot and eudicot genomes and has allowed us to propose a common ancestor with five (core gene set of 9,138) and seven proto-chromosomes (core gene set of 9,731) for monocots and eudicots, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Salse, J. et al. Reconstruction of monocotelydoneous proto-chromosomes reveals faster evolution in plants than in animals. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 14908–14913 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2844">45</a></sup>. We have integrated the <i>T. cacao</i> genome sequence information (23,529 gene models anchored) into our previous paleo-genomics analysis in order to investigate the <i>T. cacao</i> evolutionary paleo-history (Online Methods).</p><p>Using the alignment parameters and statistical tests reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Salse, J., Abrouk, M., Murat, F., Quraishi, U.M. &amp; Feuillet, C. Improved criteria and comparative genomics tool provide new insights into grass paleogenomics. Briefings Bioinf. 10, 619–630 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2857">44</a></sup>, 7,866 orthologous relationships covering 80% of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome were identified between the <i>T. cacao</i> and the <i>Arabidopsis</i>, poplar, grape, soybean and papaya genomes. The chromosome-to-chromosome orthologous relationships that were established between the <i>T. cacao</i> and the five sequenced eudicot genomes are illustrated in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig4">Figure 4a</a> and are available in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 18</a>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="T. cacao genome paleohistory."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: <i>T. cacao</i> genome paleohistory.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.736/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.736/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng736_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.736/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng736_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="854"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) <i>T. cacao</i> genome synteny. A schematic representation of the orthologs identified between <i>cacao</i> chromosomes (c1 to c10) at the center and the grape (g1 to g19), <i>Arabidopsis</i> (a1 to a5), poplar (p1 to p19), soybean (s1 to s20) and papaya (p1 to p9) chromosomes. Each line represents an orthologous gene. The seven different colors used to represent the blocks reflect the origin from the seven ancestral eudicot linkage groups. (<b>b</b>) <i>T. cacao</i> genome duplication. The seven major triplicated chromosomes groups in <i>T. cacao</i> (c1 to c10) are illustrated (colored blocks) and related with paralogous gene pairs identified between the <i>T. cacao</i> chromosomes (colored lines). The seven different colors reflect the seven ancestral eudicot linkage groups. (<b>c</b>) <i>T. cacao</i> genome evolutionary model updated from Abrouk <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Abrouk, M. et al. Palaeogenomics of plants: synteny-based modelling of extinct ancestors. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 479–487 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2930">46</a></sup>. The eudicot chromosomes are represented with a seven-color code to illustrate the evolution of segments from a common ancestor with seven protochromosomes (top). The different lineage-specific shuffling events that have shaped the structure of the six genomes during their evolution from the common paleo-hexaploid ancestor are indicated as R (for rounds of whole-genome duplication (WGD)) and F (for fusions of chromosomes). The current structure of the eudicot genomes is represented at the bottom of the figure.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.736/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Moreover, we aligned the 23,529 gene models from the <i>T. cacao</i> genome onto themselves. Seven blocks of duplicated genes (344 gene pairs) were identified and characterized in <i>T. cacao</i>, covering 64% of the genome and involving the following chromosome to chromosome (c) relationships: c2-c3-c4 (yellow), c1-c3 (blue), c1-c2-c8 (green), c6-c9 (light blue), c1-c4-c5 (purple), c5-c9-c10 (orange) and c1-c6-c7 (red) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 19</a>). We found this ancestral paleo-polyploidy event shared at orthologous positions between eudicot genomes on the following chromosome pair combinations in <i>T. cacao</i> compared to the seven ancestral triplicated chromosome groups reported in grape (g)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2961">10</a></sup>: g1-g14-g17/c2-c3-c4 (yellow), g2-g12-g15-g16/c1-c3 (blue), g3-g4-g7-g18/c1-c2-c8 (green), g4-g9-g11/c6-c9 (light blue), g5-g7-g14/c1-c4-c5 (purple), g6-g8-g13/c5-c9-c10 (orange), g10-g12-g19/c1-c6-c7 (red) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). This result confirms the paleo-hexaploid origin of the eudicot species recently reported for the grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2968">10</a></sup> and soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2972">42</a></sup> genomes. Moreover, we confirmed the known γ paleo-hexaploidization event in the <i>T. cacao</i> genome through classical Ks-based (synonymous substitution rate) data analysis between paralogous genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>).</p><p>Based on the ancestral (γ) and lineage-specific duplications (α, β) reported for eudicots, it became possible to propose an evolutionary scenario that shaped the ten <i>T. cacao</i> chromosomes from the seven chromosomes of the eudicot ancestor and, more precisely, to the 21 chromosomes of the paleo-hexaploid ancestor (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). We suggest, from the 21 chromosomes intermediate ancestor, at least 11 major chromosome fusions (referenced as 'F' in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>) to reach the actual ten-chromosome structure (compared to 30, 4, 71, 82 and 56 reported, respectively, for <i>Arabidopsis</i>, grape, poplar, soybean and papaya genomes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Abrouk, M. et al. Palaeogenomics of plants: synteny-based modelling of extinct ancestors. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 479–487 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d52119e2998">46</a></sup>.</p><p>Finally, in order to gain insight into our understanding of the molecular mechanisms driving the chromosome number reduction from the 21 chromosome ancestor intermediate to the actual 10 chromosome structure of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome, we produced heat maps scoring particular features such as CoDing Sequences (CDS), transposable element repeats for class I and II, tandem-Gaucho elements and telomeric repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). We observed a classical distribution pattern of CDS and transposon elements that were more abundant at the telomeric and centomeric regions of the chromosomes, respectively. Moreover, we identified a clear correlation between the position of chromosome fusion points (dotted rectangles) and the occurrence of telomeric repeats (telomeric remnants collocating with eight out of the eleven sites) consistent with a telomere-telomere recombination process leading to the chromosome fusion events reported previously in eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Murat, F. et al. Ancestral grass karyotype reconstruction unravels new mechanisms of genome shuffling as a source of plant evolution. Genome Res. 11, 1545–1547 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d52119e3011">47</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>We sequenced the genome of <i>T. cacao</i>, resulting in the assembly of 76% of its genome and identification of 28,798 protein-coding genes, among which 23,529 (82%) were anchored onto the ten cocoa chromosomes. A large proportion of the euchromatin of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome is likely covered by this assembly, allowing for the recovery of 97.8% of the <i>T. cacao</i> unigene resource. We found that 682 gene families are specific to <i>T. cacao</i>, as compared to <i>A. thaliana</i>, grape, soybean and poplar proteomes. Only 24% of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome consists of transposable elements, a lower percentage than in other genomes of similar size. The analysis of specific gene families that are potentially linked to cocoa qualities and disease resistance showed that particular expansion or reduction of some gene families appears to have occurred. The mapping of these gene families along the cocoa chromosomes and comparison with the genome regions involved in trait variation (QTLs) constitutes an invaluable source of candidate genes for further functional studies that aim to discover the specific genes directly involved in trait variation. This draft genome sequence will facilitate a better understanding of trait variation and will accelerate the genetic improvement of <i>T. cacao</i> through efficient marker-assisted selection and exploitation of genetic resources. Using an updated version of the Newbler software (released by Roche on 8/17/2010), we performed a second-generation assembly of the cacao genome data (ICGS Assembly 1.2). The new assembly covers 84.3% of the <i>T. cacao</i> B97-61/B2 genome, with a N50 scaffold size of 5.624 Mb and the largest scaffold of 18.20 Mb. This enhanced assembly enabled us to improve its' anchorage onto the genetic map, which now includes approximately 87% of the assembled sequences on ten pseudochromosomes. Additional details of this and further assembly improvements will be available on the ICGS website (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#Sec10">URLs</a>).</p><p>This study has highlighted the close evolutionary relationship of the <i>T. cacao</i> genome to the eudicot putative ancestor, showing a limited number of recombinations between ancestral chromosomes, as has also been observed in grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d52119e3058">10</a></sup>. <i>T. cacao</i>, which has only ten pairs of chromosomes, is easily propagated by both sexual and vegetative methods and can be transformed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Maximova, S.N. et al. Over-expression of a cacao class I chitinase gene in Theobroma cacao L. enhances resistance against the pathogen, Colletotrichum gloeosporioides. Planta 224, 740–749 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d52119e3065">48</a></sup>, and therefore, it represents a new and simple model to study the evolutionary processes, gene function, genetics and biochemistry of tree fruit crops.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">URLs.</h3><p>Cocoa statistics, <a href="http://www.icco.org/economics/market.aspx">http://www.icco.org/economics/market.aspx</a>, <a href="http://www.dropdata.org/cocoa/cocoa_prob.htm">http://www.dropdata.org/cocoa/cocoa_prob.htm</a>; ICGS website, <a href="http://cocoagendb.cirad.fr/gbrowse/cgi-bin/gbrowse/theobroma/">http://cocoagendb.cirad.fr/gbrowse/cgi-bin/gbrowse/theobroma/</a>; web tools provided by the Bioinformatics and Systems Biology of Gent, <a href="http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/webtools/Venn/">http://bioinformatics.psb.ugent.be/webtools/Venn/</a>; MUST, <a href="http://csbl1.bmb.uga.edu/ffzhou/MUST/">http://csbl1.bmb.uga.edu/ffzhou/MUST/</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">High molecular weight DNA preparation.</h3><p>High molecular weight DNA was prepared from nuclei of B97-61/B2 cocoa leaves according to previously described protocols<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ammiraju, J.S.S. et al. The Oryza bacterial artificial chromosome library resource: construction and analysis of 12 deep-coverage large-insert BAC libraries that represent the 10 genome types of the genus Oryza. Genome Res. 16, 140–147 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.736#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d52119e3124">49</a></sup>, except that the steps of filtration were replaced by five successive filtrations with nylon filters (SEFAR NITEX) having a decreasing mesh diameter: 250 μM, 100 μM, 50 μM and two times 11 μM. (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.736#MOESM2">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Construction of BAC libraries.</h3><p>Two BAC libraries were constructed from cocoa leaves. DNA was isolated from nuclei collected in agarose plugs and DNA digestions were performed with HindIII or EcoRI, followed by ligation to the pAGIBAC1 vector (a modified pIndigoBAC536Blue with an additional <i>Swa</i>I site<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ammiraju, J.S.S. et al.
